201 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Harris v. State


No. 30, September Term, 1987


Court of Appeals of Maryland


312 Md. 225; 539 A.2d 637; 1988 Md. LEXIS 43



April 5, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Circuit Court for Harford County, Brodnax Cameron, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR HARFORD COUNTY IMPOSING A DEATH SENTENCE VACATED. CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT FOR A NEW SENTENCING PROCEEDING IN CONFORMITY WITH THIS OPINION. COSTS TO BE PAID BY BALTIMORE COUNTY.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant previously entered guilty pleas to first degree murder, armed robbery, and use of a handgun in the commission of a crime of violence. The Circuit Court for Harford County (Maryland) reimposed the death sentence for the second time. Defendant challenged the second reimposition of a sentence of death.


OVERVIEW: At the sentencing hearing relevant to this appeal, the state introduced two victim impact statements. The murder victim's mother talked about how her son's death affected her own emotional health. The murder victim's brother discussed his own grief, his fear of being murdered, and the failure of the family business. He attributed this unhappiness to his brother's murder. The victim's brother also expressed his opinion of the crime itself. The court thought that both statements were clearly inadmissible because they focused on the effect that the murder had on the victim's family, rather than on defendant. Additionally, the victim's brother impermissibly commented on the nature of the crime. The court decided that the use of the victim impact statements required it to vacate the death sentence. Next, the court determined that double jeopardy did not bar the state from seeking to impose the death penalty yet again. The court rejected defendant's argument that having to undergo another death penalty hearing constituted cruel and unusual punishment. Finally, the court found no due process violation in forcing defendant to submit to a fourth capital sentencing proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court vacated defendant's death sentence and remanded the case to the trial court for commencement of a new death penalty sentencing proceeding.

 

202 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Chu v. Anne Arundel County


No. 40, September Term, 1987


Court of Appeals of Maryland


311 Md. 673; 537 A.2d 250; 1988 Md. LEXIS 30



February 16, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals, Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County, Judge James C. Cawood, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY THE PETITIONERS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner dentists sought a writ of certiorari challenging the judgment of the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland), which reversed the trial court's order requiring the return of records seized from the dentists under a search warrant in the dentists' civil action under Md. Ann. Code of 1957, art. 27, § 551(a).


OVERVIEW: In his investigation of possible fraud on dental insurers, an investigator for respondent county applied for a search warrant of the dentists' practice. No criminal proceedings were ever brought against the dentists. The trial court ordered return of documents seized in the search, but the intermediate appellate court reversed. On appeal, the dentists argued that § 551(a), which provided for the return of property taken under a search warrant that was unsupported by probable cause, incorporated the federal exclusionary rule, and that information in the warrant application was improperly obtained. The court affirmed, holding that § 551 did not contain an exclusionary rule. The court noted that: (1) § 551 contained no clear textual support for the dentists' argument that it contained an exclusionary rule; that there was no evidence of a legislative purpose to absorb the federal exclusionary rule; (2) a proposed amendment to § 551 that included an exclusionary rule was stricken before enactment; and (3) the legislature did not modify § 551 after the intermediate appellate court, in a prior opinion, found that § 551 did not include an exclusionary rule.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the intermediate appellate court's judgment that reversed the trial court's order requiring that documents seized from the dentists under a search warrant be returned to the dentists.

 

203 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Lee v. State


No. 14, September Term, 1987


Court of Appeals of Maryland


311 Md. 642; 537 A.2d 235; 1988 Md. LEXIS 31



February 16, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court of Montgomery County - Judge John J. Mitchell).



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY PETITIONERS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants challenged the judgment of the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court of Montgomery County) (Maryland), which convicted them both of attempted second degree murder, robbery with a dangerous and deadly weapon, and use of a handgun in a crime of violence, all arising out of a holdup and shooting. The court issued a certiorari on the issue of a reasonable search and seizure.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were arrested by police officers who contemporaneously seized a handgun and other evidence. Following an evidentiary hearing defendants' pretrial motion to suppress was denied. The court determined that there was a "seizure" of defendants' persons, within the meaning of the Fourth Amendment, when the Special Assignment Teams left their surveillance positions and charged with weapons drawn while identifying themselves as police officers and ordering the five young men to lie prone on a basketball court. These circumstances amounted to a show of official authority such that a reasonable person would have believed that he was not free to leave. The court held that the "hard take down," based on reasonable suspicion, was not an unconstitutional seizure; the determinative element in the balancing process was that the police reasonably suspected that defendants were armed and dangerous. The court also held that ordering defendant to lie on the basketball court did not convert the investigative stop into a seizure requiring probable cause.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court.

 

204 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Hoey v. State


No. 11 September Term, 1986


Court of Appeals of Maryland


311 Md. 473; 536 A.2d 622; 1988 Md. LEXIS 16



February 5, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended February 5, 1988. As Amended February 17, 1988. As Amended February 22, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court for Baltimore County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals, Austin W. Brizendine, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE COUNTY AFFIRMED. APPELLANT TO PAY THE COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with attempted storehouse burning, possession of a Molotov cocktail, and wearing or carrying a concealed and dangerous weapon. The Circuit Court for Baltimore County (Maryland) found defendant guilty of each charge. Defendant brought an appeal.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that his confessions and the psychiatrist's statements were improperly admitted and that it was not constitutional to place the burden of proof on the defendant to show that he was not criminally responsible. The court held that mental deficiency, standing alone, was insufficient to make the confession involuntary because there was sufficient testimony presented at the suppression hearing to conclude that the trial judge did not err in finding that defendant was mentally capable of understanding what he was saying. The court rejected defendant's contentions that he did not voluntarily waive his Miranda rights, holding that there was no police overreaching. The court held that the state was not required to disclose the existence of the psychiatrist because the psychiatrist was not consulted by the state. Rather all tests conducted by the psychiatrist were made as part of the psychiatrist's duties in treating defendant. The court held that while the state retained the burden of proof on all elements of the crimes, it was constitutionally permissible to require defendant to prove his lack of criminal responsibility.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions.

 

205 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Yangming Marine Transport Corp. v. Revon Products U.S.A., Inc.


No. 21, September Term, 1986


Court of Appeals of Maryland


311 Md. 496; 536 A.2d 633; 1988 Md. LEXIS 18; 88 A.L.R.4th 451; 1988 AMC 2690



February 5, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Circuit Court for Howard County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals, Robert F. Fischer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR HOWARD COUNTY REVERSED. APPELLEE TO PAY COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff shipping company appealed from the order of the Circuit Court for Howard County (Maryland), which granted defendant corporation's motion to dismiss the shipping company's action to recover shipping costs from the corporation.


OVERVIEW: The trial court dismissed the shipping company's action on the grounds that the shipping company, as a foreign corporation neither registered nor qualified to do business in the state, was barred from maintaining the action pursuant to Md. Code Ann., Corps. & Ass'ns § 7-301. On appeal, the court held that § 7-301 barred an unqualified or unregistered foreign corporation from suing in Maryland courts only if the corporation was doing such a substantial amount of localized business in the state that the corporation could be deemed present in the state. The court held that the § 7-301 doing business test required a significantly greater amount of local activity in the state than would be necessary for Maryland to assert personal jurisdiction over the corporation under the state long arm statute. The court noted that the shipping company did not pay taxes, maintain property, inventory, or research and development facilities, or engage in management functions in the state. The company had no state telephone listing, bank accounts, or employees. Therefore, the company did not do business in the state for the purpose of § 7-301, and thus it was error to dismiss the action.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed.

 

206 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


State v. Hogg


No. 94, September Term, 1987


Court of Appeals of Maryland


311 Md. 446; 535 A.2d 923; 1988 Md. LEXIS 11



January 14, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals, H. Chester Goudy, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: ORDER OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR ANNE ARUNDEL COUNTY DENYING THE MOTION TO DISMISS IS AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY THE STATE OF MARYLAND.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the Maryland Deposit Insurance Fund (MDIF) and the State of Maryland (state), sought review of an order from the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County (Maryland), which denied appellants' motion to dismiss a counterclaim filed by appellees, officers and directors of the Maryland Savings-Share Insurance Corporation (MSSIC), in an action by MDIF and the state for damages caused by appellees' breach of duty to MSSIC.


OVERVIEW: The MSSIC was merged into the MDIF, which was a specially created state agency. Under the pressures of a financial crisis in the savings and loan industry, the merger allowed the State to accept MSSIC's insurance liabilities in an effort to restore public confidence and to acquire MSSIC's assets. The MDIF and the State filed an action against the officers and directors of the MSSIC for breaches of duty to MSSIC. The officers and directors filed a counterclaim that the harm complained of resulted from the negligence of state employees in the Division of Savings and Loan Associations (DSL). The appellants filed a motion to dismiss the counter complaint on the grounds of sovereign immunity. The circuit court refused to dismiss the counterclaim against the state. Appellants claimed on appeal that the counterclaim was barred by absolute immunity. The court rejected appellants' claim. The court ruled that the counterclaim was for recoupment and that claim against the officers and directors was assigned to MDIF during the merger. Accordingly, the state was the real party in interest, and the officers and directors could seek recoupment against the state in their counterclaim.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the circuit court's denial of MDIF and the state's motion to dismiss the counterclaim by the officers and directors of MSSIC.

 

207 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Montgomery County v. McNeece


No. 118, September Term, 1985


Court of Appeals of Maryland


311 Md. 194; 533 A.2d 671; 1987 Md. LEXIS 301



November 24, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Circuit Court for Montgomery County, Judge Stanley B. Frosh, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY MONTGOMERY COUNTY, MARYLAND.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county sought review of the decision of the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland), which found in favor of appellee firefighter in the firefighter's action after he received a promotion, but no increase in pay.


OVERVIEW: A firefighter sought administrative relief from the county fire and rescue commission after he received a promotion, but no increase in pay. The commission denied the firefighter relief, and he appealed to the merit system protection board, which also denied him relief. Subsequently, the firefighter appealed to the circuit court, which entered an order that reversed the administrative action and directed the commission to award the firefighter an increase in salary. Then the county filed a notice for in banc review, and the firefighter countered with a motion to dismiss. The circuit court in banc granted the motion and dismissed the county's appeal. The county next filed an unsuccessful petition to the court of special appeals for a writ of certiorari. The court of special appeals granted the county's petition for certiorari and affirmed the lower court's decision. On subsequent appellate review, the court held that although the in banc court erred in holding that it was without jurisdiction to entertain the appeal, its decision was final and unappealable, and the court of special appeals correctly dismissed the county's appeal of the determination of the in banc review.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the appellate court's dismissal of the county's appeal from the in bancs decision.

 

208 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Rice v. State


No. 23, September Term, 1987


Court of Appeals of Maryland


311 Md. 116; 532 A.2d 1357; 1987 Md. LEXIS 297; 75 A.L.R.4th 73



November 5, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Montgomery County - Judge L. Leonard Ruben).



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED, WITH COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland) that convicted him of theft of property pursuant to § 342(a) and (c) of Maryland's consolidated theft statute (statute), Md. Code Ann. art. 27, § 342(a) and (c). Defendant was also convicted of burglary, two counts of robbery with a dangerous and deadly weapon, and two counts of use of a handgun in a crime of violence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that it was error to fail to instruct the jury he could be convicted only if all jurors agreed unanimously that he had committed all the elements of larceny under Md. Code Ann. art. 27, § 342(a) or all the elements of possession of stolen property under Md. Code Ann. art. 27, § 342(c). He reasoned that without unanimity as to the specific section he allegedly violated, he was denied the jury unanimity guaranteed by the Maryland Constitution. The court affirmed the judgment. The court explained that when comparing the statutory alternatives, conduct, mental state, attendant circumstances, and result had to be considered, and that if substantial differences resulted from such a comparison, the statutory alternatives might have to be regarded as separate crimes for jury unanimity purposes. The court compared these elements of the two subsections involved and concluded that they were not autonomous offenses but rather one crime defined two ways. The court declared that the law required unanimity only in the verdict, not in the rationale upon which the verdict was based.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction.

 

209 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Department of Transp. v. Armacost


No. 5, September Term, 1987


Court of Appeals of Maryland


311 Md. 64; 532 A.2d 1056; 1987 Md. LEXIS 293



November 3, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court for Carroll County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals, Donald J. Gilmore, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED; CASE REMANDED TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR CARROLL COUNTY FOR ENTRY OF A DECLARATORY JUDGMENT CONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION; COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLEES.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellees, county commissioners and others (county), filed a petition for declaratory judgment and an interlocutory injunction against appellant state departments (state). The petition challenged the constitutionality of the application of Maryland's Vehicle Emissions Inspection Program (VEIP) to the county. The Circuit Court for Carroll County (Maryland) enjoined the enforcement of the VEIP in the county, and the state appealed.


OVERVIEW: The lower court found that Md. Code Ann., Transp. §§ 23-202, 23-207 of the VEIP, Md. Code Ann., Transp. §§ 23-201 to 23-208, constituted an unlawful delegation of power from the legislature to an administrative agency, in violation of Md. Const. Decl. Rts. art. 8. The court disagreed and reversed the lower court's judgment. The court noted that broad delegations of legislative authority to administrative agencies was particularly appropriate in the areas of public health and safety. The court rejected the county's argument that art. 8 required a much stricter application of the separation of powers doctrine than was applied in cases arising under the federal constitution. Anticipating that the federal law provisions would be modified on a continuing basis, the state legislature delegated authority to the administrative agencies to maintain a VEIP to the extent required by federal law. If the legislature had waited to conform its VEIP legislation to new federal standards, it would have risked losing federal grant money that hinged on compliance with federal law. Moreover, federal law provided ample guidance to the states in establishing and administering the inspection programs.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's decision, which invalidated the VEIP legislation. The court remanded the case to the lower court for entry of a declaratory judgment in favor of the state.

 

210 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Random House, Inc. v. Comptroller of Treasury


No. 9, September Term, 1987


Court of Appeals of Maryland


310 Md. 696; 531 A.2d 683; 1987 Md. LEXIS 290



October 8, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Circuit Court for Baltimore City pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals, David Ross, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE CITY AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review of a judgment of the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland), which affirmed a tax court decision in favor of appellee Comptroller of the Treasury (comptroller) in the taxpayer's appeal of an assessment of business income for three years, including subsidiary rights income on an apportionable basis pursuant to Md. Ann. Code art. 81, § 316(c) (1957, 1980 Rep. Vol., 1986 Cum Supp.).


OVERVIEW: The principal offices of the taxpayer, a book publisher, were located in New York, and it maintained a warehouse in Maryland. The taxpayer objected to comptroller's assessment by increasing the business income to be apportioned, over that reported, by including income from subsidiary rights, such as magazine, film, and broadcasting rights. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in favor of the comptroller. The taxpayer asserted that if subsidiary rights income was to be included in the apportionable basis pursuant to § 316(c), then both the sales and property factors should be adjusted to reflect the subsidiary rights. The court held that the taxpayer did not show that the income apportioned to the state under comptroller's assessment was disproportionate to a constitutionally significant degree to the business transacted by the taxpayer in the state. The court found that the comptroller was not compelled to value the subsidiary rights under the method proposed by the taxpayer. The court noted that the taxpayer did not carry subsidiary rights on its balance sheet and did not submit proof as to the total cost of subsidiary rights with respect to its literary works.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in favor of the comptroller in the taxpayer's appeal of the assessment of its business income.

 

211 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


State v. Davis


No. 142, September Term, 1986


Court of Appeals of Maryland


310 Md. 611; 530 A.2d 1223; 1987 Md. LEXIS 283



September 21, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Baltimore County -- Judge Cullen H. Hormes).



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS AFFIRMED IN PART AND REVERSED IN PART. CASE REMANDED TO THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS FOR THE ENTRY OF A JUDGMENT AFFIRMING THE JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE COUNTY. COSTS TO BE PAID BY DREXEL OTTO DAVIS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State of Maryland filed a petition for certiorari seeking review of the judgment of the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland), which vacated the judgment of the trial court sentencing defendant to life imprisonment without parole under Md. Code Ann. § 643B(b) (Supp. 1986). Defendant was convicted of housebreaking for the fourth time, and he filed a conditional cross-petition for certiorari challenging the convictions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with housebreaking and was convicted after a jury trial. Because defendant had a prior conviction for burglary and four prior convictions for housebreaking, which were crimes of violence under Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 643B(b) (Supp. 1986), he was sentenced to life imprisonment without parole. The appellate court affirmed the conviction but vacated the sentence. On appeal, the court reversed the appellate court's judgment vacating the sentence. The court ruled that there was no jurisdictional defect underlying one of the prior housebreaking convictions because the informations adequately described the criminal act alleged. The court ruled that defendant's guilty pleas to the prior housebreaking and burglary charges were made knowingly and voluntarily. The court stated that housebreaking was a serious and potentially dangerous and violent crime. Moreover, the court noted that several states had harsher maximum penalties for a first-time housebreaking offense than Maryland and that four to six states imposed sentences comparable to Maryland's for repeat offenders. Thus, the court held that defendant's sentence was proportionate under the Eighth Amendment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court affirming defendant's convictions for housebreaking. However, the court reversed the judgment of the appellate court vacating the sentence of life imprisonment without parole. The court remanded the case to the appellate court with instructions to enter judgment affirming in toto the judgment of the trial court.

 

212 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Jones v. State


Nos. 72, 122, September Term, 1985


Court of Appeals of Maryland


310 Md. 569; 530 A.2d 743; 1987 Md. LEXIS 315



September 16, 1987 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] JUDGMENT AFFIRMED WITH COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Circuit Court for Baltimore County (Maryland) convicted defendant for first-degree murder, attempted murder, assault with intent to murder, robbery with a deadly weapon, and use of a handgun in a crime of violence. The trial court sentenced defendant to death for the two murder convictions. The defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant entered the victims' home, killed the husband and his stepdaughter, took two rings from the wife, and shot her three times. On appeal from the conviction and death sentence, the court held that whether the wife was under the influence of drugs at the time of the murders was irrelevant to defendant's claim that the photographic identification by the wife was impermissibly suggestive. The court held that the prosecutor's closing arguments regarding defendant's coldness and his demeanor were not improper. The court held that the prosecution's failure to turn over statements by the wife was not error because two of the documents were police notes of interviews, not statements, and the third was given to defense counsel and counsel was given the opportunity to recall the wife to the stand, which he did not do. The court held that the trial court was not required to accept defendant's waiver of jury sentencing prior to voir dire. The court held that the jury instruction in the penalty phase, which had a listing of aggravating and mitigating factors for the jury to check yes or no, was not prejudicial because the jury was given an instruction not to use a mathematical formula.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for murder, attempted murder, assault with intent to murder, robbery, and use of a handgun in a crime of violence. The court affirmed the sentence of death imposed for the murder conviction.

Wildermuth v. State


Nos. 2, 7, September Term, 1987


Court of Appeals of Maryland


310 Md. 496; 530 A.2d 275; 1987 Md. LEXIS 279



September 10, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County, pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals, Bruce C. Williams, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT IN NO. 2 (ADV.), SEPT. TERM, 1987 (WILDERMUTH) REVERSED. CASE REMANDED FOR PROCEEDINGS CONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION. COSTS TO BE PAID BY ANNE ARUNDEL COUNTY.

JUDGMENT IN NO. 7 (ADV.), SEPT. TERM, 1987 (MCKOY) AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's challenged the decision of the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County (Maryland), which convicted defendants of child abuse following a trial where children were allowed to testify by means of closed circuit television.


OVERVIEW: Defendants argued that Md. Code Ann. Cts. & Jud. Proc. § 9-102(a) was unconstitutional because it violated the confrontation clause of U.S. Const. amend. VI. The court rejected defendants' contentions. However, because the court agreed that the threshold showing essential to the invocation of § 9-102 was not made, the court held that the conviction of the defendant who contested the issue at the trial level should have been reversed and his case remanded for further proceedings. Since the other defendant failed to contest the issue at the trial level, his conviction was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of the defendant who failed to contest the issue of testimony by closed circuit television at the trial level but reversed the conviction of the second defendant who did contest the issue and remanded his case for further proceedings.

 

214 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Hargrove v. Board of Trustees


No. 119, September Term, 1985


Court of Appeals of Maryland


310 Md. 406; 529 A.2d 1372; 1987 Md. LEXIS 277



September 2, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals, Edward A. DeWaters, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED, WITH COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff judge appealed from the decision of the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland), which found that defendant retirement board acted properly in applying provisions of the Judicial Pension Plan, Md. Code Ann. art. 73B, § 56(c)(1) (repealed 1983) to calculate the allowable pension for the judge, which turned out to be nothing due to an offset provision.


OVERVIEW: The judge challenge offset provisions in the Judicial Pension Plan because it reduced his pension if he accepted employment that was paid for by municipal, county, state or federal funds. The court found the provision to be valid, and it affirmed the judgment of the circuit court ruling in favor of the retirement board. No suspect classification or fundamental right was involved, so the court applied a rational basis test rather than strict scrutiny. It found no equal protection violations, although the offset provision applied to judges but not all other local government employees. The judge failed to demonstrate a total lack of any rational basis underlying § 56(c)(1), which sought to prevent double dipping from judges that might hold more than one form of employment. Accordingly, there was no violation of constitutional law although his the pension offset resulted in his pension being reduced to nothing due to other employment on his part.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the circuit court in favor of the retirement board.

Maryland Port Admin. v. QC Corp.


No. 119, September Term, 1986


Court of Appeals of Maryland


310 Md. 379; 529 A.2d 829; 1987 Md. LEXIS 274



August 21, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Motion for Reconsideration Denied October 5, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Baltimore City - Chief Judge Robert I. H. Hammerman).



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS ON THE INVERSE CONDEMNATION CLAIM ASSERTED IN COUNT III OF THE COMPLAINT IS REVERSED. IN ALL OTHER RESPECTS THE JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS IS AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY QC CORPORATION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Maryland Port Administration, sought review of a judgment from the Maryland Court of Special Appeals, which reversed the trial court and ruled in favor of appellee property owner on an inverse condemnation claim.


OVERVIEW: The property owner was a chemical processing corporation located near a hazardous waste landfill. It alleged that it was forced to cease its operations at that site because chrome particles from the landfill blew onto its property. The concentration of chrome in the air at the chemical processing plant did not exceed permissible amounts under applicable regulations. The court reversed the inverse condemnation judgment, holding that the property owner had not suffered a significant degree of interference with the business. The court held that where there was no physical invasion of the property, no taking was effected by consequential damages; rather, the impact on the owner's property had to be special to it and of a high degree.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's judgment and held that there was no taking.

 

216 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Scott v. State


No. 105, September Term, 1985


Court of Appeals of Maryland


310 Md. 277; 529 A.2d 340; 1987 Md. LEXIS 270



August 5, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at Scott v. State, 2003 Md. App. LEXIS 45 (Md. Ct. Spec. App., Apr. 30, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Circuit Court for Baltimore City, John Carroll Byrnes, Judge.
Scott v. State, 297 Md. 235, 465 A.2d 1126, 1983 Md. LEXIS 298 (1983)



DISPOSITION: DEATH SENTENCE VACATED. CASE REMANDED FOR A NEW SENTENCING PROCEEDING UNDER § 413 OF ARTICLE 27. COSTS TO BE PAID BY THE MAYOR AND CITY COUNCIL OF BALTIMORE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision by the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland) reimposing a death penalty originally imposed by the trial court after defendant was convicted of premeditated murder. The death penalty had been vacated by the court of appeals. Defendant argued that the trial court committed numerous errors during the sentencing hearing including its refusal to instruct the jury regarding the state's burden.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the lower court's decision reimposing the death sentence originally pronounced after the defendant's conviction for premeditated murder. The court held that, under Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 413(h), the trial court erred in declining to instruct the jury on the state's burden of demonstrating that the aggravating circumstances outweighed the mitigating circumstances. The court held that the trial court did not err in prohibiting the defendant from contesting the qualifications of an expert witness based on the witness's numerous appearances on behalf of the state in capital cases. Defendant's challenge of the witness went to the weight that should be afforded the testimony of the witness rather than his qualifications as a witness. The court held that defendant's prior criminal convictions were admissible during the sentencing proceeding under Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 413(c)(1). Section 413(c)(1)(iii) referred to convictions before the sentencing hearing itself. The court held that out-of-court comments made by the state's attorney did not violate due process principles. The court vacated the death sentence and remanded for new sentencing proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court vacated defendant's death sentence and remanded for a new sentencing proceeding. The trial court erred in refusing to instruct the jury that the state bore the burden of demonstrating that the aggravating circumstances outweighed the mitigating circumstances during defendant's sentencing. However, defendant was not entitled to challenge the qualifications of an expert witness who had previously appeared on behalf of the state.

 

217 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Comptroller of Treasury v. World Inns, Inc.


No. 147, September Term, 1985


Court of Appeals of Maryland


310 Md. 154; 528 A.2d 477; 1987 Md. LEXIS 257



July 28, 1987 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE COUNTY REVERSED AND CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT FOR ENTRY OF JUDGMENT AFFIRMING THE ORDER OF THE MARYLAND TAX COURT. APPELLEES TO PAY THE COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Comptroller of the Treasury (comptroller) sought review of a decision of the Circuit Court for Baltimore County (Maryland), which reversed the tax court's order and held in favor of appellee motel regarding an exemption from the Maryland Retail Sales Tax Act (Act), Md. Ann. Code art. 81, §§ 324-371 (1957, 1980 Repl. Vol. and 1986 Cum. Supp.) for a government employee's transactions paid for with personal funds for government business.


OVERVIEW: The comptroller levied an assessment against the motel because it failed to collect retail sales tax on rooms rented to government employees. The motel challenged the assessment and claimed an exemption based on Md. Ann. Code art. 81, § 326(a), (f) (1957, 1980 Repl. Vol. and 1986 Cum. Supp.). A comptroller's hearing officer and the tax court affirmed the assessment. When the circuit court reversed the decision, the comptroller appealed. The court granted certiorari on its own motion prior the appellate court's consideration, reversed the decision, and remanded the case to the circuit court to enter a judgment affirming the tax court's order. The court held that a tax exemption was proper when the legal incidence of a state tax fell directly on the government and on the purchaser when a state statute required a seller to collect a sales tax from a purchaser. The employees were purchasers within the meaning of the Act because they dealt directly with the motel and paid for the rooms with their personal funds, so the government was not a party to the arrangement. Because the legal incidence of the tax fell on the employees and not the government, the exemption did not apply.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded the case to the circuit court for entry of a judgment affirming the order of the tax court, which ruled in favor of the comptroller in the motel's action regarding a federal employee's exemption from retail sales tax.

 

218 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Mills v. State


Nos. 22, 79, September Term 1985


Court of Appeals of Maryland


310 Md. 33; 527 A.2d 3; 1987 Md. LEXIS 247



June 25, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Allegany County, J. Frederick Sharer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was tried before a jury for first degree murder and sentenced to death for the stabbing death of his cell mate while he was incarcerated at a correctional facility. Defendant appealed from the judgment of conviction and sentence entered by the Circuit Court for Allegheny County (Maryland).


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the capital punishment statute was unconstitutionally mandatory. The court affirmed. The capital punishment statute was not unconstitutionally mandatory. Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 413(h)(2) (1957) (repealed 1982) provided that, if the jury found that mitigating factors did not outweigh aggravating factors, the sentence had to be death. Former Maryland Rule 772A provided a verdict form which instructed the jurors that if one or more aggravating circumstances were present and if no mitigating circumstances were found, the sentence must be death. The form thus required the jurors to unanimously decide that there were no mitigating factors. A unanimous decision that there were no mitigating factors did not automatically result in a death sentence, as the jurors were required to unanimously determine that the death sentence should be imposed. If the jury was unable to decide whether to impose the death sentence, the jury was to be dismissed and the court had to impose a life sentence. Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 413(k)(2). Thus, if there was no unanimity that no mitigating circumstances existed, the jurors were required to engage in a weighing process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that convicted defendant of first degree murder and sentenced him to death.

 

219 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


State v. Standifur


No. 136, September Term, 1985


Court of Appeals of Maryland


310 Md. 3; 526 A.2d 955; 1987 Md. LEXIS 244



June 19, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals, Circuit Court for Cecil County, Judge Donaldson C. Cole, Jr. and Judge Edward D. E. Rollins, Jr.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENTS OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY PETITIONER.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of the judgment of the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland), which reversed defendants' convictions because a witness' statement was erroneously introduced into evidence under the statement against penal interest exception to the hearsay rule.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted of robbing a home and selling a gun taken in the robbery. The man who bought the gun told the police that defendants had sold it to him. At the time of trial, the witness was no longer available so his statement was admitted into evidence as a statement against penal interest, an exception to the hearsay rule. The trial court convicted both defendants. The appellate court reversed. The state appealed. The court affirmed. The court concluded that the witness' statement should not have been admitted as a declaration against penal interest. The court concluded that the totality of circumstances under which the statement was made militated against a finding of the requisite reliability. The court found that a motive of personal gain, while not invariably fatal to the presumed reliability of a declaration against penal interest, was an important factor to be considered. The court held that the statement was not sufficiently reliable to be admitted for the purpose of inculpating an accused in a criminal case.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the reversal of defendants' convictions.

Boyds Civic Asso. v. Montgomery County Council


Nos. 62, 100, September Term, 1986 (Two Cases)


Court of Appeals of Maryland


309 Md. 683; 526 A.2d 598; 1987 Md. LEXIS 242



June 8, 1987 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] JUDGMENT IN NO. 62 REVERSED AND CASE REMANDED TO THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS FOR PASSAGE OF AN ORDER REVERSING THE ORDER OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR MONTGOMERY COUNTY DISMISSING THE PROCEEDING AND REMANDING THE CASE TO THAT COURT FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS CONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION; RESPONDENTS TO PAY THE COSTS. JUDGMENT IN NO. 100 AFFIRMED; PETITIONERS TO PAY THE COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated cases, petitioner citizens, property owners and a civic association, challenged the order of the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland), which affirmed the trial court's order that dismissed their declaratory judgment action against respondents, county council and area planning commission (county), and intervenor respondent stone quarry, for failure to present a justiciable controversy.


OVERVIEW: The county designated land as suitable for a Mineral Recovery Zone under the county zoning ordinance in its master plan. The citizens filed suit for declaratory judgment that the county had failed to provide the necessary notice and public hearings, which therefore invalidated the designation. The trial court dismissed the claim as an unjustifiable controversy, and the appellate court affirmed. On further appeal, the court reversed. The county's alleged failure to provide notice and hearings constituted a prior violation and not an uncertain future violation. Neighboring property owners, such as the citizens, had a justiciable interest in the amending of the master plan to recommend a Mineral Resource Recovery zoning. The court affirmed the companion case order of the appellate court that the amendment to the master plan by the county was not a "contested case" under the Administrative Procedure Act, specifically Md. Code. Ann., State Gov't § 10-215(a) (1984), but disagreed with the reasoning. The amendment was not a "contested case" under § 10-215(a) because the county was not a state agency under Md. Code Ann., State Gov't § 10-201(b), and the action was mandated by statute.


OUTCOME: In the first case, the court reversed the judgment of the appellate court which had dismissed the declaratory judgment claim. It remanded to the trial court for further proceedings and required the county to pay the costs of appeal. In the second case, the court affirmed the order of the appellate court which found that the Administrative Procedure Act was not applicable to the county and ordered the citizens to pay the costs of appeal.

Motor Vehicle Admin. v. Lindsay


No. 82, September Term, 1986


Court of Appeals of Maryland


309 Md. 557; 525 A.2d 1051; 1987 Md. LEXIS 234



June 2, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Circuit Court for Montgomery County, L. Leonard Ruben, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR MONTGOMERY COUNTY REVERSED; CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT FOR ENTRY OF JUDGMENT AFFIRMING THE AGENCY DETERMINATION; COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLEE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Motor Vehicle Administration (MVA) challenged a judgment of the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland), which found a decision of the MVA to suspend appellee petitioner's driver's license to be arbitrary and capricious and remanded with instructions to restrict the suspension to allow the petitioner to drive to work and to alcohol treatment.


OVERVIEW: Following the petitioner's conviction of driving a motor vehicle while intoxicated, the MVA, after a hearing, suspended his driver's license for 90 days. The court reversed the decision of the circuit court and held as follows: (1) the MVA was a proper party to appeal the circuit court's decision under Md. Code Ann. Cts. & Jud. Proc. § 12-305 (1984) because the legislature intended that the MVA would be a party defendant in appeals taken to the circuit court by persons aggrieved by an agency decision under the Maryland Vehicle Law; (2) the MVA could have revoked the petitioner's license based on his driving record; (3) the hearing officer decided to exercise the discretion allowed by Md. Ann. Code Transp. § 16-405(a) (Cum. Supp. 1986) and suspend the petitioner's license for a relatively brief period; (4) manifestly, the agency decision was not arbitrary or capricious when the record contained substantial evidence supporting its determination; and (5) the hearing officer was not obligated to issue a restricted license for employment or alcohol treatment purposes.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the circuit court's order and remanded the case for affirmance of the MVA's decision to suspend the petitioner's license for 90-days.

Ogrinz v. James

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorneys sought review of a judgment from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland), which declared Chapter 281 of the Acts of 1986 (Act) constitutional in all of the challenged respects and determined that a certain insurance company was lawfully created.


OVERVIEW: The attorneys filed a declaratory judgment action in the lower court, mounting a broad-based challenge to the constitutionality of Md. Ann. The lower court declared §§ 566-574 constitutional in all of the challenged respects and determined that the insurance company had been lawfully created. The court held that it was a valid exercise of the police power, to stabilize and to create or increase competition in the lawyers' professional liability insurance market.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court that declared the Act constitutional in all of the challenged respects and determined that a certain insurance company was lawfully created.

Parren v. State


No. 91, September Term, 1986


Court of Appeals of Maryland


309 Md. 260; 523 A.2d 597; 1987 Md. LEXIS 218



April 6, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals, Circuit Court for Baltimore City, Judge Robert B. Watts.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS REVERSED. CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT WITH DIRECTION TO REVERSE THE JUDGMENTS OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE CITY AND TO REMAND THE CASE FOR A NEW TRIAL. COSTS TO BE PAID BY THE MAYOR AND CITY COUNCIL OF BALTIMORE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were convicted by a jury at a joint trial of certain felonies and misdemeanors charged in a number of indictments returned against them. The Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) affirmed. Defendants petitioned for certiorari and the state cross-petitioned for certiorari.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were involved in an incident while incarcerated and thereafter convicted for their conduct. The trial judge granted defendants their request to represent themselves. The appellate court noted that the trial court characterized the right that it gave as one of self-representation and that it conducted a quasi waiver-of-counsel inquiry. The appellate court concluded that it was actually a form of representation in which defendants participated in their own defense while also retaining the assistance of counsel and opined that it was a question of degree whether a trial court allowed pro se representation or hybrid representation. The court held that there were only two rights of representation, by counsel or pro se. Those were independent rights that could not be asserted simultaneously. The court concluded that defendants' background, experience, and conduct indicated that defendants were not novices to the criminal justice system, but that defendants were not told by the trial court or by counsel about the penalties involved. Noncompliance with this requirement of Md. R. Crim. Causes 4-215 rendered their waivers of counsel ineffective because the rule was mandatory.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded for a new trial.

Chase v. State


No. 114, September Term, 1986


Court of Appeals of Maryland


309 Md. 224; 522 A.2d 1348; 1987 Md. LEXIS 215



April 3, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Prince George's County Judge Arthur M. Ahalt).



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENTS AFFIRMED; COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Maryland Court of Special Appeals, which affirmed the probation hearing court's denial of defendant's motion to dismiss for admitting evidence that was excluded in defendant's criminal proceeding in the probation revocation proceeding. The court of appeals held that the exclusionary rule did not apply in defendant's revocation of probation hearing.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to robbery and was placed on probation with the condition that he obey all laws. Later, defendant was seen selling drugs by two police officers. Although drug possession charges were not prosecuted against defendant because the trial court found defendant's arrest was illegal and thus, the evidence of drugs on him while arrested was inadmissible, the probation hearing court allowed that evidence to be admitted at his probation revocation hearing. The court of appeals affirmed the probation hearing court's ruling; the court affirmed the judgments of the court of appeals and the revocation hearing court. The court found that defendant's possible absence at the probation hearing in no way influenced the probation court's ruling. The court also ruled that evidence that was suppressed in a criminal proceeding need not be suppressed in a probation revocation proceeding because the probation proceeding was a civil action However, the court ruled due process required a finding that the arresting officers' actions were pursued in good faith. Because there was no lack of good faith on the part of the officers in this case, the evidence was properly admitted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals, and found that the revocation hearing court did not err in refusing to apply the exclusionary rule at defendant's probation revocation hearing. The court ruled that defendant's possible absence at the hearing did not affect the hearing court's ruling, and that the exclusionary rule did not apply to probation revocation proceedings where actions of the police officers were in good faith.

Board of Education v. Prince George's County Educators' Asso.


No. 55 September Term, 1985


Court of Appeals of Maryland


309 Md. 85; 522 A.2d 931; 1987 Md. LEXIS 209; 125 L.R.R.M. 2923



March 26, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Prince George's County - Judge William H., Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS AFFIRMED. PETITIONER TO PAY COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant board of education (board) sought review of the judgment of the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland), which ruled in favor of appellee union and held that the award in favor of the board should have been vacated.


OVERVIEW: A union and the board of education (board) entered into a collective bargaining agreement. The union later filed a grievance against the board, alleging that the changes in the driver education program pursuant to a board resolution violated the collective bargaining agreement by unilaterally reducing the rank and pay of driver education teachers without cause. The arbitrator applied the successorship doctrine to hold that the driver education school was a separate entity from the board and, thus, not bound by the terms of the collective bargaining agreement. The court held that the appellate court correctly ruled that the award in favor of the board should have been vacated. Under Maryland law, the board was the employer of the driver education teachers. There was no successor employer within the meaning of the successorship doctrine. The school was nothing more than an administrative division of the board itself. The arbitrator made a palpable mistake of law by concluding that the board and the driver education school were separate and independent entities. It was an error apparent on the face of the award which rendered the award unenforceable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

Public Service Com. v. Maryland People's Counsel


No. 130, September Term, 1985


Court of Appeals of Maryland


309 Md. 1; 522 A.2d 369; 1987 Md. LEXIS 200



March 20, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from The Circuit Court for Baltimore City pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE CITY REVERSED. PEOPLE'S COUNSEL TO PAY THE COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Public Service Commission challenged a Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland) judgment on remand that held regulations promulgated by the commission establishing minimal procedural requirements before terminating a residential customer's service unconstitutional. The commission contended appellee people's counsel lacked standing and the regulations were not ripe for review. The court granted certiorari for direct review.


OVERVIEW: People's counsel filed a complaint challenging the constitutionality of regulations promulgated by the commission which established minimal procedural requirements that had to be followed before a utility could terminate a residential customer. People's counsel asserted that the regulations violated due process because they failed to require an adequate hearing before service was terminated. The trial court found the regulations unconstitutional because they did not require a pretermination hearing, and the court granted certiorari before intermediate appellate court review. The commission contended that the people's counsel lacked standing under Md. Ann. Code art. 78, § 89 (1957), and that the mere promulgation of discretionary regulations did not present a ripe issue for review. The court concluded that people's counsel had standing to bring suit under § 89 to protect the interests of residential utility customers. The court, however, reversed the judgment of the trial court, finding that the regulations were not ripe for review because the application of the regulations was discretionary and there would be only a minimal hardship on residential customers by postponing review.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court that held the commission's regulations were unconstitutional because the regulations were not ripe for review. The court determined that the people's counsel had standing to bring the suit. Costs were taxed against the people's counsel.

G. Heileman Brewing Co. v. Stroh Brewery Co.


Misc. No. 13, September Term, 1986


Court of Appeals of Maryland


308 Md. 746; 521 A.2d 1225; 1987 Md. LEXIS 199



March 13, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certified question of Law from the United States Court of Appeals for the Fourth Circuit, Sam J. Ervin III, Judge.



DISPOSITION: QUESTIONS OF LAW ANSWERED AS HEREIN SET FORTH; COSTS TO BE EQUALLY DIVIDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court was required by the United States Court of Appeals for the Fourth Circuit to interpret the Maryland Beer Franchise Fair Dealing Act, Md. Ann. Code. art. 2B, §§ 203A-203G.


OVERVIEW: In a dispute between appellant beer distributor and appellee brewer over the termination of distribution agreement, a federal appellate court required the court to interpret the Maryland Beer Franchise Fair Dealing Act (Act), Md. Ann. Code. art. 2B, §§ 203A-203G. The court held that the Act applied to an agreement between a franchisor and a franchisee, where the franchisee was also a manufacturer of beer, in competition with the franchisor. Thus, the court held that the notice provision contained in Md. Ann. Code. art. 2B, §§ 203D applied to the termination of the agreement between appellant and appellee. The court held that whether good cause existed under Md. Ann. Code art. 2B, § 203B was not solely a question of law because it involved a judgmental process, requiring the application on a case by case basis of established factual findings. The court held that a finding of good cause would not be overturned unless the trial court failed to meet the substantial evidence test.


OUTCOME: The court held that the Maryland Beer Franchise Fair Dealing Act (Act) applied to an agreement between a franchisor and a franchisee, where the franchisee was also a manufacturer of beer, in competition with the franchisor. Whether good cause existed under the Act was not solely a question of law because it involved a judgmental process, requiring the application on a case by case basis of established factual findings.

Snyder v. Glusing


No. 107, September Term, 1986


Court of Appeals of Maryland


308 Md. 411; 520 A.2d 349; 1987 Md. LEXIS 178



January 29, 1987, Opinion


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Motion for Reconsideration Denied November 5, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: Order October 23, 1986. Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore COunty, Leonard S. Jacobson, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant unsuccessful candidate sought review of the judgment of the Circuit Court for Baltimore County (Maryland), which found in favor of appellee successful candidate in the unsuccessful candidate's suit contesting the sample ballot distributed by the successful candidate.


OVERVIEW: The unsuccessful candidate contested the election results. The unsuccessful candidate alleged that the distribution of a sample ballot purporting to be from the official party was in violation of the election law. The trial court found that there had been an election law violation but that the unsuccessful candidate failed to prove, by clear and convincing evidence, that the result might have been different, but for the violation of Md. Ann. Code art. 33, § 26-16(a)(7). On appeal, the court reversed and remanded. The court held that the use of the sample ballot violated Md. Ann. Code art. 33, § 26-16(a)(7) and that the trial court erred in requiring the unsuccessful candidate to show that the violation "would" have changed the election result. On remand, the trial court found in favor of the successful candidate, and the court affirmed. In light of the conflicting inferences raised by the evidence, the court could not say that the trial judge was clearly erroneous in concluding that the unsuccessful candidate had not proven, by clear and convincing evidence, that, had there been no election law violation, the outcome might have been changed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

229 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Prince George's County v. Fitzhugh


Misc. No. 5, September Term, 1986


Court of Appeals of Maryland


308 Md. 384; 519 A.2d 1285; 1987 Md. LEXIS 174



January 28, 1987 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] CERTIFIED QUESTIONS ANSWERED AS ABOVE SET FORTH. COSTS TO BE PAID ONE-HALF BY APPELLANT AND ONE-HALF BY APPELLEES.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Questions were certified by the United States District Court for the District of Maryland regarding a wrongful death action brought against defendant county by plaintiffs, decedent child's parents. The questions involved the validity of the partial waiver of governmental immunity found in Prince George's County, Md., Charter § 1013 (1982), based on Md. Ann. Code art. 25A, § 5(CC) (1976).


OVERVIEW: In 1982, the voters amended § 1013 of the Prince George's County Charter to limit the county's waiver of governmental immunity by permitting tort actions against it only in cases for which its officers, agents, and employees may be liable. In the underlying federal diversity action, police officers were found negligent in an accidental shooting death but were found to have acted within the scope of their employment. The court found that the 1982 amendment was invalid in its attempt to waive only some of the county's immunity because this was not permissible under the plain language of Md. Ann. Code art. 25A, § 5(CC). The court further found that § 5(CC) was constitutional. Thus, the court held that when the county waived governmental immunity, it was required to do it so that the county could be sued in tort actions in the same manner that any private person could be sued. Consequently, the court answered "no" to the question regarding whether the county's waiver was limited. The court found the second question immaterial because, whether the 1982 amendment was severed or nullified in its entirety, the result would still be unlimited liability for the county.


OUTCOME: The court answered "no" to the question of whether the county had successfully retained some of its governmental tort immunity. The court found that the second question did not have to be answered because the result for the county was the same either way, that of unlimited liability.

Department of Natural Resources v. Welsh


No. 28, September Term, 1986


Court of Appeals of Maryland


308 Md. 54; 521 A.2d 313; 1986 Md. LEXIS 331



November 24, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Motion for Reconsideration Filed December 23, 1986. Opinion Modified; Motion Denied March 5, 1987. *


* Reporter's Note: The opinion was modified after it appeared in the Advance Sheets.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals, Circuit Court for Allegany County, Judge Gary G. Leasure.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED; APPELLANT TO PAY THE COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Department of Natural Resources (department) challenged the judgment of the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) which reversed the trial court's judgment and held that the doctrine of sovereign immunity did not prevent appellee landowner from bringing a quiet title action and that a prior condemnation action was void as to the landowner's interest.


OVERVIEW: The landowner's predecessors in interest obtained 33 acres of land from the grantor. The grantor then gave 1,000 acres away, which eventually came into a couple's possession. Through a condemnation action in which the couple were the only named defendants, the department obtained the 1,000 acres, in which the landowner's 33 acres was included. The landowner filed a quiet title action against the department. The trial court held that the department acquired the landowner's 33 acres in the condemnation action. The appellate court reversed. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the doctrine of sovereign immunity did not prevent the landowner from bringing a suit to quiet title against the department because the landowner alleged that it took his property without just compensation in violation of the federal and state constitutions. The department did not acquire title to the landowner's property in the earlier condemnation action because the landowner was not a defendant in that action.


OUTCOME: The judgment for the landowner in his quiet title action against the department was affirmed.

 

232 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Brosan v. Cochran


No. 25, September Term, 1986


Court of Appeals of Maryland


307 Md. 662; 516 A.2d 970; 1986 Md. LEXIS 318



November 10, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Ann Arundel County - Judge Bruce C. Williams).



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED, WITH COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, Superintendent of the Maryland State Police Department, sought review of a decision by the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County (Maryland), which granted injunctive and declaratory relief to appellees, attorney and his former client who was a drunk driving suspect that had been denied a private breathalyzer test from his attorney. The court granted certiorari on its own motion prior to a decision by the lower appellate court.


OVERVIEW: The client was arrested for drunk driving and, under the superintendent's order, was permitted one phone call to his attorney but no face-to-face communication was allowed. The attorney and the client challenged the superintendent's order and were granted a preliminary injunction enjoining enforcement of the order. The superintendent issued a new order allowing face-to-face consultations but prohibited counsel from administering a private breath test prior to the client's election to take or refuse the state's test. The trial court permanently enjoined the enforcement of the second order. The court affirmed and held that: (1) consultation with an attorney was not grounds to exceed the two-hour limit imposed by state law; (2) communication between attorney and client was not limited to verbal communication, included the taking of a private chemical test, and, where requested, was part of the process constitutionally due to a drunk driving suspect; and (3) the decision was limited to drunk driving cases where the decision whether to submit to the state's test was of critical importance and where it did not substantially interfere with the timely administration of the state's test.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's grant of injunctive and declaratory relief to the attorney and the client.

 

233 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Jones v. State


No. 17, September Term, 1986


Court of Appeals of Maryland


307 Md. 449; 514 A.2d 1219; 1986 Md. LEXIS 296



September 30, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Montgomery County - Judge John J. Mitchell).



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS REVERSED. CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT WITH DIRECTION TO VACATE APPELLANT'S SENTENCE AND TO REMAND TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR MONTGOMERY COUNTY FOR RESENTENCING CONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION. COSTS TO BE PAID BY MONTGOMERY COUNTY, MARYLAND.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland), which affirmed his conviction and sentence for robbery. Defendant alleged the trial court erred in imposing a greater sentence than had been originally imposed and reversed on appeal without a finding of identifiable conduct occurring after the original sentence was imposed as required by Md. Code Ann. Cts. & Jud. Proc. § 12-702(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant's original conviction and sentence was reversed by the appeals court. Another jury sitting in the circuit court convicted defendant of the same offense. At sentencing, the trial judge's attention was drawn to the fact that defendant's record listed a number of criminal offenses that he had committed before his first trial but of which he had not been convicted until after it. Based on the convictions, the trial court imposed a greater sentence than the original one. The court reversed the sentence and remanded. The court held as follows: (1) as found in § 12-702(b)(2), one of the conditions that had to be met for an increased sentence after a retrial of a criminal case was that the reasons for the increased sentence had to be based on objective information concerning identifiable conduct on the part of defendant occurring after the original sentence was imposed; (2) defendant's "identifiable conduct" occurred before his "original sentence" was imposed; and (3) convictions for crimes committed prior to the original trial was not "conduct of defendant" for purposes of imposing an increased sentence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appeals court and remanded the case with directions to vacate defendant's sentence and to remand the case to the circuit court for resentencing.

 

234 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Houghton v. County Comm'rs of Kent County


No. 76, September Term, 1985


Court of Appeals of Maryland


307 Md. 216; 513 A.2d 291; 1986 Md. LEXIS 279



August 22, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Appeal from the Circuit Court for Kent County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals, J. Owen Wise, Judge.



DISPOSITION: MOTION FOR RECONSIDERATION DENIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court dismissed the appeal of the trial court's judgment dismissing the complaint of plaintiffs, husband and wife, against defendant county. Plaintiffs filed a motion for reconsideration.


OVERVIEW: The trial court granted the county's motion to dismiss the couple's complaint but did not include the word "judgment" in the order. The appeal, which was filed when the decision was published, was dismissed because it was not filed within the time prescribed by the rules. The motion for reconsideration claimed that the appeal was unfairly dismissed because the court's holding that the dismissal order was a final order even though it did not contain the word "judgment" was novel and should only have been applied prospectively. The court held that (1) the holding that an unqualified order granting a motion to dismiss was a final and appealable order was not novel, (2) the order was final because it did not provide for amendment of the complaint and the effect was to put plaintiffs out of court and deny them the means of prosecuting their case, (3) plaintiffs had a duty to remain aware of the progress of their case and their actual knowledge was immaterial, and (4) the federal "unique circumstances" doctrine was not extended to afford relief because the proper course was to submit the issue to the Standing Committee on Rules of Practice and Procedure.


OUTCOME: The court denied plaintiff's motion for reconsideration of an order dismissing their appeal of an order that dismissed their complaint against the county.

 

235 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Nordheimer v. Montgomery County


No. 142, September Term, 1984


Court of Appeals of Maryland


307 Md. 85; 512 A.2d 379; 1986 Md. LEXIS 268



July 25, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. Irma S. Raker, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR MONTGOMERY COUNTY AFFIRMED IN PART AND VACATED IN PART, AND CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS NOT INCONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION. APPELLANTS TO PAY COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers appealed a decision from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland), pursuant to a writ of certiorari, on their claims for refunds of transfer taxes paid to defendant Montgomery County and an injunction against requiring future payment of transfer taxes in connection with the conversion of rental property into a condominium, as required under Montgomery Co., Md. Code § 52-21(h) (1981).


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers contended the transfer tax ordinance violated provisions of Md. Code Ann., Real Prop. §§ 11-120(b) and 11-127(c), and the equal protection clause because a like tax did not apply to cooperative units. The court first held that no cause of action existed against the county for refund of or a declaratory judgment concerning condominium transfer taxes which had already been paid, regardless of the validity of the transfer tax ordinance. Once the tax had been paid, there was no common law action for the recovery of the tax and no special statutory provision had been enacted to allow such a refund. In reviewing the disputed tax, the court held that the statute, which barred the county from enacting an ordinance which would impose a burden on a condominium that was not imposed on all other property of similar character, did not apply to the state. The state specifically authorized the county to impose the precise condominium transfer tax in question. The court also held that as long as the classifications made in imposing a tax were not utterly arbitrary, the tax statute met the rational basis test for equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court held that the taxing statute was valid, but it remanded the matter for modification of the declaratory order with respect to those transfers on which the tax had already been paid to reflect the holding that there was no cause of action with respect to those taxes.

Maryland-National Capital Park & Planning Com. v. Crawford


Nos. 110, 121, September Term, 1984


Court of Appeals of Maryland


307 Md. 1; 511 A.2d 1079; 1986 Md. LEXIS 263; 41 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P36,420



July 16, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Prince George's County, Perry G. Bowen).

Circuit Court for Prince George's County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals, Perry G. Bowen, Jr. Judge.



DISPOSITION: IN NO. 110, JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS AFFIRMED. PETITIONERS TO PAY COSTS. IN NO. 121, JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR PRINCE GEORGE'S COUNTY VACATED, AND CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS NOT INCONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION. COSTS TO BE EQUALLY DIVIDED BETWEEN THE PETITIONERS AND RESPONDENT'S COUNSEL.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee filed suit for damages and injunctive relief against defendant state park commission (employer), based on race discrimination. The Circuit Court for Prince George's County (Maryland) found in favor of the employee. The employer appealed, and the Court of Special Appeals affirmed. The employer sought certiorari.


OVERVIEW: The employer entered into an agreement with the Equal Employment Opportunity Commission (EEOC), under which it would hire or promote minority persons, unless the white candidate was clearly superior. The employee, who was white, sought transfer. Although the employee was determined to be clearly superior, another applicant, who was black, was given the position. The employee filed an administrative grievance. When the grievance was denied, the employee appealed. While the appeal was pending, the employee filed suit. The court held that because the administrative remedies were completed prior to trial, and because the trial court only ruled on preservation of evidence matters prior to the completion of the administrative process, there was no error. The court held that the employee was not required to file a complaint with the EEOC or the state employment commission prior to filing her suit under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The court held that the trial court's finding that the employee was clearly superior was not clearly erroneous. The court held that the trial court had jurisdiction to award attorney fees for the appeal, but that the bonus added to the hourly fees was not justified.


OUTCOME: The court granted certiorari and affirmed the appellate court's judgment, which had affirmed the trial court's judgment in favor of the employee on damages and injunctive relief. The court vacated the attorney fee award and remanded the case to the trial court for further consideration.

Lerner v. Lerner


No. 133, September Term, 1985


Court of Appeals of Maryland


306 Md. 771; 511 A.2d 501; 1986 Md. LEXIS 264



July 15, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals. William M. Cave, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR MONTGOMERY COUNTY AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY THE APPELLANTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a corporation, its majority shareholder, and two directors, appealed from an order of the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland), which enjoined, pending a decision on the merits, a reverse stock split by which the majority shareholder sought to cash out the interest of plaintiff, the corporation's minority shareholder and brother to the majority shareholder. The court issued a writ of certiorari.


OVERVIEW: A corporation's minority shareholder filed suit against the majority shareholder, his brother, alleging he diverted real estate opportunities away from the company, amongst other claims. The majority shareholder then undertook to freeze his brother out as a stockholder by having a reverse stock split. The minority shareholder then filed an action to enjoin the freezeout. The trial court entered an order enjoining, pending a decision on the merits, the reverse stock split. The court issued a writ of certiorari, following defendants' appeal. The issue before the court was whether the chancellor below abused his discretion in preliminarily enjoining the freezeout, pending the ultimate decision on the merits. The court found that the freezeout, if allowed to proceed, would destroy the minority shareholder's alleged right to be and remain a shareholder in the company, and thus was an irreparable injury in the context of preliminary injunctions. As to other complications arising from the freezeout, the court held they could be avoided by maintaining the status quo until the merits were decided. A flexible interplay among all the various factors supported a preliminary injunction.


OUTCOME: Judgment of the lower court preliminary enjoining, pending a decision on the merits, a freezeout of the minority shareholder's interest by way of a reverse stock split, was affirmed.
